  26→\n    27→class HashUtil {\n    28→ private:\n    29→  static const hash_t PRIME_FACTOR = 10000019;\n    30→\n    31→ public:\n    32→  static inline auto HashBytes(const char *bytes, size_t The key insight is that the input value gets hashed first—the code uses HashUtil::HashValue on the Value object, not the raw integer. So I need to figure out what hash(1) actually produces through that hashing function.\n\nWorking backwards from the cardinality formula: if E = 0.79402 * 2^r = 1665180, then r = 21. This means the register value should be 21, which comes from counting leading zeros in the hash value. So __builtin_clzll(hash(1)) must equal 20, giving us 20 leading zero bits.\n\nBut I'm realizing there might be a mismatch between my understanding of how the leading zeros are being counted versus how the implementation actually works. Let me examine the bitset logic more carefully to see if I'm using the right bit operation. for hash(1), the binary representation must have its first 1 bit at position 43 from the right to yield 20 leading zeros.\n\nIn standard HyperLogLog, counting leading zeros across all 64 bits works fine. But for Presto's implementation, I need to reconsider how I'm measuring position. The issue is that I should only count leading zeros within the 64-p bit range, not treat it as a full 64-bit value. When I extract remaining_bits via hash >> n_bits, I'm working with a smaller effective bit width, so the position of the first 1 bit needs to be interpreted relative to that narrower context, not the full 64-bit space. For n_bits=1, remaining_bits occupies bits 0-62 conceptually, and if it's zero, the position should be 63; otherwise I need to account for the shift when calculating where the first set bit appears.\n\nThe core issue is understanding what \"remaining bits\" represents. In standard HyperLogLog, after extracting the k-bit bucket index, the leftover bits are examined to find the run length. Since I'm computing `remaining_bits = hash >> n_bits`, this shifts away the index portion, leaving a value where significant bits sit in the lower positions. To find the position of the first set bit in this shifted value, I could use `ctzll(remaining_bits) + 1` to get the run length directly from the trailing zeros.\n\nTesting this approach with the 262144 example: `remaining_bits = 131072`, `ctzll(131072) = 17`, so position becomes 18. This gives `dense = 18 & 15 = 2` and `overflow = 18 >> 4 = 1`, both correct. Yet my current implementation uses `__builtin_clzll(remaining_bits) + 1` with leading zeros instead. The discrepancy might explain why the basic HyperLogLog works but the dense variant doesn't—I may have incorrectly switched from trailing to leading zeros when adapting the algorithm.\n\nLooking at the basic implementation, it relies on `ComputeBinary` and `PositionOfLeftmostOne`, which iterates through the bitset from MSB to LSB, effectively counting leading zeros. If this version passes EdgeTest2, then the leading-zero approach must be correct for the overall algorithm, suggesting my dense variant's issue lies elsewhere in how I'm computing or applying the position value. are the results diverging?\n\nLet me trace through the Presto path with 262144 and n_bits=1:\n- hash = 262144\n- bucket_idx = 262144 & 1 = 0\n- remaining_bits = 262144 >> 1 = 131072\n- __builtin_clzll(131072) = 47 (since 131072 = 2^17)\n- leftmost_one_pos = 48\n- dense = 48 & 15 = 0\n- overflow = 48 >> 4 = 3\n\nThe test expects dense=2, overflow=1, so something's off. I think the problem is that when I shift the hash right by n_bits, I'm not accounting for the fact that I've already consumed those bits for the bucket index.\n\nActually, the bucket index comes from the low-order bits: bucket_idx = hash & ((1 << n_bits) - 1). After removing them with remaining_bits = hash >> n_bits, what's left should be the higher-order bits. For remaining_bits = 131072, the leading zeros are bits 47-63, but the significant bit is at position 17. The __builtin_clzll gives 47, which seems right, but the test expects position 18 instead—suggesting it wants trailing zeros counted, not leading ones.\n\nI need to use __builtin_ctzll for Presto instead. But this creates a puzzle: the basic HyperLogLog also relies on leading zeros and EdgeTest2 passes. When n_bits = 0, the bucket index calculation becomes bucket_idx = h & 0 = 0, meaning all bits should go toward the position calculation rather than the bucket index.\n\nFor EdgeTest2 with value 1, if the register outputs 21, then clzll(h) must equal 20, placing the first set bit at position 43. This suggests the hash is something like 2^43 plus additional bits—reasonable for a pseudo-random hash.\n\nThe issue arises with Presto and value 262144 where n_bits=1. Using the same hash function that returns 262144 directly creates a mismatch in how the bucket index is computed.\n\nWhen I extract the lowest bit (262144 & 1 = 0) and shift right (262144 >> 1 = 131072), then count leading zeros (__builtin_clzll(131072) = 47), the leftmost one position becomes 48. But the expected value is 18, indicating the hash functions differ between implementations. The Presto CalculateHash for int64_t returns the value directly, whereas the basic HyperLogLog implementation uses bustub::HashUtil::HashValue on a Value object wrapping the int64_t.\n\nThis means Presto's hash(262144) = 262144 itself, while basic HyperLogLog produces a different hashed value. For the remaining bits, if I count trailing zeros in 131072 instead of leading zeros, I get 17 + 1 = 18, which matches the expected position. So Presto appears to use trailing zeros for the position calculation, unlike the basic HyperLogLog which uses leading zeros.\n\nWait—maybe I've been wrong about the basic implementation too. EdgeTest2 passes, but that could be coincidental. If I switch both to trailing zeros by iterating from LSB to MSB through the bitset, that might be the correct approach across the
